Whitney anukramaс╣З─л

[N─Бr─Бyaс╣Зa.тАФtrayastriс╣Е┼Ыat. p─Бrс╣гс╣Зis┼лktam; p─Бuruс╣гam; brahmaprak─Б┼Ыis┼лktam (31, 32. s─Бkс╣г─Бtparabrahmaprak─Б┼Ыiny─Бu). ─Бnuс╣гс╣нubham: 1-4, 7, 8. triс╣гс╣нubh; 6, 11. jagat─л; 28. bhurig bс╣Ыhat─л.]

Whitney

Comment

Found also (except vss. 8, 18, 23, 28) in P─Бipp. xvi. (in the verse-order 1-7, 26, 27, 9-12, 17, 15, 13, 14, 16, 22, 19, 24, 25, 20, 21, 30, 29, 31, 32, 33). Quoted (vs. 1) in V─Бit. 37. 19, together with the other puruс╣гas┼лkta (xix. 6), in the puruс╣гamedha or human sacrifice; not noticed in K─Бu┼Ы.

Translations

Translated: Muir, v. 375 (nearly all); Ludwig, p. 398; Scherman, Philosophische Hymnen, p. 41 (nearly all); Deussen, Geschichte, i. 1. 265; Henry, 5, 45; Griffith, ii. 6.

Griffith

Purusha, Primeval Man or humanity personified

Whitney
Translation
  1. By whom were brought the two heels of a man (p├║ruс╣гa)? by whom was
    his flesh put together? by whom his two ankle-joints (gulph├б)? by whom
    his cunning (p├й┼Ыana) fingers? by whom his apertures? by whom his (two)
    uchlakh├бs in the midst? who [put together] his footing
    (pratiс╣гс╣нh─Б╠Б)?
Notes

The pada-text divides utре░┼Ыlakh─Б├║, as if there were such a word as
┼Ыlakha. Ppp. reads, instead of keno тАЩchlakh─Бu, keno тАЩcchinaс╣Б ko;
also, in a, p─Бrс╣гс╣З─лy ─Бbhс╣Ыte p─Бuruс╣гasya; and, in c, pe┼Ыin─лс╣г.
P├й┼Ыana is more literally ‘arranging, adorning.’

Griffith

Who framed the heels of Purusha? Who fashioned the flesh of him? Who formed and fixed his ankles? Who made the openings and well-moulded fingers? Who gave him foot-soles and a central station?

Translation

nirс╣Ыtijan╠Дghe ni dadhuс╕е; also, in d, saс╣Бdh─лm ┼л ca j─Бn─Б. In b
it has again p─Бuruс╣гasya.

режрек рдХрддрд┐ рджреЗрд╡рд╛рдГ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. How many gods [and] which were they, who gathered (ci) the
    breast, the neck-bones of man? how many disposed the two teats? who the
    two collar-bones (? kaphoс╕Н├б)? how many gathered the shoulder-bones
    (pl.)? how many the ribs?
Notes

Ppp. has again p─Бuruс╣гasya in b. In c it reads ni dadhuс╣г kaс╣г
kapol─Бu. The mss. are extremely discordant as to the form of the word
which our edition gives as kaph─Бuс╕Н─Б├║; that is the reading of Bp.W.D.;
P.M.R.s.m. have kapheс╕Н─Б├║, I. kaph─Бujh─Б├║, E.O.R.p.m.T.K. kaphoс╕Н─Б├║
(which accordingly has the most authority in its favor тМКall SPP’s mss.
read soтМЛ); several saс╣Бhit─Б-mss. (P.M.T.O.p.m.R.s.m.) have k├бс╣г before
it. The meaning given is, of course, conjectural only; ‘collar-bone’ is
Ludwig’s guess, and seems to suit the connection (though that is a
rather weak ground of preference) better than the ‘perhaps elbow’ of
the Pet. Lexx. The Anukr. takes no notice of the lacking syllable in
a.

Griffith

Who and how many were those Gods who fastened the chest of Purusha and neck together? How many fixed his breasts? Who formed his elbows? How many joined together ribs and shoulders?

Who put together both his arms and said, Let him show manly strength? Who and what God was he who set the shoulderblades upon the trunk?

Translation
  1. Who bored out the seven apertures in his headтАФthese ears, the
    nostrils, the eyes, the mouth? in the might of whose conquest (vijay├б)
    in many places quadrupeds [and] bipeds go their way.
Notes

Bp. reads at the end y─Б╠Бman. Ppp. puts n─Бsike after cakс╣гaс╣З─л, and
reads in c vij─Бyasya mahamani, and at the end yom┼лn. The Anukr.
does not heed that d is a triс╣гс╣нubh p─Бda.

Griffith

Who pierced the seven openings in the head? Who made these ears, these nostrils, eyes, and mouth, Through whose surpassing might in all directions bipeds and quadrupeds have power of motion?

Whitney
Translation
  1. Since in his jaws he put his ample (pur┼лc─л╠Б) tongue, then attached
    (adhi-┼Ыri) [to it] great voice; he rolls greatly on among
    existences, clothing himself in the waters: who indeed understands that?
Notes

With c is to be compared ix. 10. 11 d; the irregularity of the
p─Бda is not noticed by the Anukr. Ppp. reads instead of c: sa ─Б
var─лvarti mahin─Б vyomaс╣Б: avas─Бnaс╣г kata cit pra veda. Our text ought to
read var─лvartti.

Griffith

He set within the jaws the tongue that reaches far, and thereon placed Speech the mighty Goddess. He wanders to and fro mid living creatures, robed in the waters. Who hath understood it?

Whitney
Translation
  1. Which was that god who [produced] his brain, his forehead, his
    hindhead (? kak─Б╠Бс╣нik─Б), who first his skull, who, having gathered a
    gathering in man’s jaws, ascended to heaven?
Notes

D. reads city├бm in c; all the mss. agree in ruroha, although
rur├│ha is obviously required. тМКOtherwise Henry.тМЛ The verse, as noted
above, is wanting in Ppp.

Griffith

Who was he, first, of all the Gods who fashioned his skull and brain and occiput and forehead, The pile that Purusha’s two jaws supported? Who was that God who mounted up to heaven?

Whence bringeth mighty Purusha both pleasant and unpleasant things, Of varied sort, sleep, and alarm, fatigue, enjoyments and de- lights?

Translation
  1. Who disposed in him waters, moving apart, much moving, produced for
    river-running, strong (t─лvr├б), ruddy, red, dark and turbid, upward,
    downward, crosswise in man?

is very stupidly defined as jagat─л by the Anukr.

Translation
  1. With what did he cover this earth? with what did he surround the
    sky? by what is man a match for (abh├н) mountains in greatness? by
    what, for deeds?
Notes

This verse, as noted above, is wanting in Ppp.

Griffith

Through whom did he bedeck the earth, through whom did he encompass heaven? Whose might made Purusha surpass the mountains and created things?

Translation
  1. Wherewith does he dwell upon (? anu-kс╣гi) the gods? wherewith
    [upon] the people of the god-folk? wherewith this other asterism?
    whereby is authority (kс╣гatr├б) called real (s├бt)?
Notes

The sense here is very obscure, and the rendering mechanical. тМКGriffith
suggests that the point may lie in using nakс╣гatram as if it were na
kс╣гatram, ’non-power,’ in opposition to kс╣гatram in d.тМЛ Ppp. has,
for b, kena dev─лr ajanayad di┼Ыaс╕е. The meter requires in a
kс╣гyati, as the forms are written in some texts. The Anukr. takes no
notice of the irregularity.

Griffith

Through whom doth he abide with Gods? Through whom with the Celestial Tribes? Why is this other called a star? Why is this called the Real Power?

реирен рддрджреНрд╡рд╛ рдЕрдерд░реНрд╡рдгрдГ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Verily that head of Atharvan [is] a god-vessel, pressed together;
    breath defends that, the head, food, also mind.
Notes

For samubj─лta, as said of a ko┼Ыa, compare ix. 3. 20 above. Ppp.
reads pr─Бс╣Зo тАШbhi in c, and ┼Ыr─лm for ┼Ыiras in d. The three
nouns in d might be nom. instead of accus.

Griffith

That is indeed Atharvan’s head, the well-closed casket of the Gods. Spirit and Food and Vital Air protect that head from injury.

реирео рдКрд░реНрдзреНрд╡реЛ рдиреБ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Was he now created upward (┼лrdhv├б)? [or] was he now created
    crosswise? did man grow unto (─Б-bh┼л) all the quarters?тАФhe who knoweth
    the br├бhman’s stronghold, from which man is [so] called.
Notes

The meaning of the protracted final syllables here is unquestionable,
although it has been overlooked by both Muir and Ludwig. The cases of
protraction call out much treatment from the Pr─Бt.: see the rules i. 70,
97, 105; iv. 6, 120, 121, and the notes upon them. The mss. differ in
regard to accenting or leaving unaccented the final syllable of b;
nor is the usage of either RV. or AV. sufficiently settled to determine
which reading ought to be preferred. Puruс╣гa in this verse and the
sequel seems to approach its later meaning of ‘supernal Person or
Spirit.’ There is no apparent connection between the two halves of the
verse: for the second, see vs. 30. The whole verse is wanting in Ppp.
The Anukr. should have called it a prast─Бrapan╠Дkti; bhurig bс╣Ыhat─л is
purely mechanical (10 + 11: 8 + 8 = 37).

Griffith

Stationed on high, Purusha hath pervaded all regions spread aloft and stretched transversely. He who knows Brahma’s cattle, yea, the fort whence Purusha is named,

Whitney
Translation
  1. Whoever indeed knoweth that br├бhman’s stronghold, covered with
    amс╣Ы╠БtaтАФunto him both the br├бhman and the Brahmans have given sight,
    breath, progeny.
Notes

The verse is found also in TA. (i. 27. 3), which reads pur─лm at end of
b, brahm─Б for br─Бhm─Б┼Ы in b, and ─Бyuс╕е k─лrtim for cakс╣гuс╕е
pr─Бс╣Зam in d (the accentuation is corrupt and worthless through the
whole verse). Ppp. has also in d ─Бyuс╣г for cakс╣гuс╕е, and at the end
dadhuс╕е.

Griffith

Yea, knows that fort of Brahma girt about with immortality, Brahma and Brahmas have bestowed sight, progeny, and life on him.

Translation
  1. Him verily sight doth not desert, nor breath, before old age, who
    knoweth the br├бhman’s stronghold (p├║r), from which man (p├║ruс╣гa) is
    [so] called.
Notes

The latter half-verse is identical with 28 c, d. Ppp. reads puraс╕е
at end of b, and yasm─Бt in d.

Griffith

Sight leaves him not, breath quits not him before life’s natural decay, Who knows the fort of Brahma, yea, the fort whence Purusha is named.

Griffith

Men deep in lore of Brahma know that Animated Being which Dwells in the golden treasure-chest that hath three spokes and three supports.

Griffith

Brahma hath passed within the fort, the golden castle; ne’er subdued, Bright with excessive brilliancy, compassed with glory round about.
